<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Bacteria</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the microorganisms. For the genus, see <a href="/wiki/Bacterium_(genus)" title="Bacterium (genus)">Bacterium (genus)</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Bacteria_(disambiguation)" title="Bacteria (disambiguation)">Bacteria (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A domain of prokaryotes – single celled organisms without a nucleus</div>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p>Eubacteria <small>Woese &amp; Fox, 1977</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese1977_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woese1977-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<p><b>Bacteria</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪər/: 'ear' in 'near'">ɪər</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-bacteria.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/En-us-bacteria.ogg" title="En-us-bacteria.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>; common noun <b>bacteria</b>, singular <b>bacterium</b>) are a type of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">biological cell</a>. They constitute a large <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domain</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>. Typically a few <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometres</a> in length, bacteria have a <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cell_structure#Cell_morphology" title="Bacterial cell structure">number of shapes</a>, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Coccus" title="Coccus">spheres</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bacillus_(shape)" title="Bacillus (shape)">rods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spiral_bacteria" title="Spiral bacteria">spirals</a>. Bacteria were among the first life forms to appear on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and are present in most of its <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>. Bacteria inhabit soil, water, <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">acidic hot springs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and the deep portions of <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a>. Bacteria also live in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic" title="Symbiotic">symbiotic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasitic" title="Parasitic">parasitic</a> relationships with plants and animals. Most bacteria have not been characterised, and only about half of the <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_phyla" title="Bacterial phyla">bacterial phyla</a> have species that can be <a href="/wiki/Microbiological_culture" title="Microbiological culture">grown</a> in the laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rappe_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rappe-5">[5]</a></sup> The study of bacteria is known as <a href="/wiki/Bacteriology" title="Bacteriology">bacteriology</a>, a branch of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>.
</p><p>There are typically 40 million bacterial <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> in a gram of soil and a million bacterial cells in a millilitre of <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>. There are approximately 5×10<sup>30</sup> bacteria on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9618454_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9618454-6">[6]</a></sup> forming a <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> which exceeds that of all plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Bacteria are vital in many stages of the <a href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycle" title="Nutrient cycle">nutrient cycle</a> by recycling nutrients such as the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">fixation of nitrogen</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. The nutrient cycle includes the <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cadaver" title="Cadaver">dead bodies</a> and bacteria are responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Putrefaction" title="Putrefaction">putrefaction</a> stage in this process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In the biological communities surrounding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" title="Hydrothermal vents">hydrothermal vents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_seeps" title="Cold seeps">cold seeps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> bacteria provide the nutrients needed to sustain life by converting dissolved compounds, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulphide" title="Hydrogen sulphide">hydrogen sulphide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, to energy. Data reported by researchers in October 2012 was published in March 2013. It was suggested that bacteria thrive in the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a>, which with a depth of up to 11 kilometres is the deepest known part of the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20130317_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20130317-10">[10]</a></sup> Other researchers reported related studies that microbes thrive inside rocks up to 580 metres below the sea floor under 2.6 kilometres of ocean off the coast of the northwestern United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130314_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130314-11">[11]</a></sup> According to one of the researchers, "You can find microbes everywhere—they're extremely adaptable to conditions, and survive wherever they are."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The famous notion that bacterial cells in the human body outnumber human cells by a factor of 10:1 has been debunked. There are approximately 39 trillion bacterial cells in the <a href="/wiki/Human_microbiota" title="Human microbiota">human microbiota</a> as personified by a "reference" 70 kg male 170 cm tall, whereas there are 30 trillion human <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> in the body. This means that although they do have the upper hand in actual numbers, it is only by 30%, and not 900%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest number exist in the <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a>, and a large number on the <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The vast majority of the bacteria in the body are rendered harmless by the protective effects of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>, though many are <a href="/wiki/Probiotic" title="Probiotic">beneficial</a>, particularly in the gut flora. However several species of bacteria are <a href="/wiki/Pathogenic_bacteria" title="Pathogenic bacteria">pathogenic</a> and cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a>. The most common fatal bacterial diseases are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_infection" title="Respiratory infection">respiratory infections</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> alone killing about 2 million people per year, mostly in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> are used to treat <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">bacterial infections</a> and are also used in farming, making <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> a growing problem. In industry, bacteria are important in <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a> and the breakdown of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_spills" title="Oil spills">oil spills</a>, the production of <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a>, the recovery of gold, palladium, copper and other metals in the mining sector,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, and the manufacture of antibiotics and other chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once regarded as <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> constituting the class <i>Schizomycetes</i>, bacteria are now classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes">prokaryotes</a>. Unlike cells of animals and other <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, bacterial cells do not contain a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> and rarely harbour <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">membrane-bound</a> <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>. Although the term <i>bacteria</i> traditionally included all prokaryotes, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">scientific classification</a> changed after the discovery in the 1990s that prokaryotes consist of two very different groups of organisms that <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> from an <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">ancient common ancestor</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">evolutionary domains</a> are called <i>Bacteria</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woese-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>

font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div></div></div><div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div><div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><div style="float: right;"><span style="font-size:85%;">(<a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">view</a> • <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Life_timeline" title="Template talk:Life timeline">discuss</a>)</span></div><div style="float: left;"><small><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">million years</a></small></div><br/><span style="float:right;margin-right:0.5em"><img alt="The image above contains clickable links" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="133" height="27" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/18px-Interactive_icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/27px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/36px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 2x" title="The image above contains clickable links" width="18"/></span><div style="float: left;"><small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>bacteria</i> is the plural of the <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">bacterium</i></i>, which is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latinisation_(literature)" title="Latinisation (literature)">latinisation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">βακτήριον</span> (<i>bakterion</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> the diminutive of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B2%CE%B1%CE%BA%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%81%CE%AF%CE%B1" title="wikt:βακτηρία">βακτηρία</a> (<i>bakteria</i>), meaning "staff, cane",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> because the first ones to be discovered were <a href="/wiki/Bacillus_(shape)" title="Bacillus (shape)">rod-shaped</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_early_evolution">Origin and early evolution</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_evolution" title="Timeline of evolution">Timeline of evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history of life</a></div>
<p>The ancestors of modern bacteria were unicellular microorganisms that were the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">first forms of life</a> to appear on Earth, about 4 billion years ago. For about 3 billion years, most organisms were microscopic, and bacteria and archaea were the dominant forms of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Although bacterial <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> exist, such as <a href="/wiki/Stromatolite" title="Stromatolite">stromatolites</a>, their lack of distinctive <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> prevents them from being used to examine the history of bacterial evolution, or to date the time of origin of a particular bacterial species. However, gene sequences can be used to reconstruct the bacterial <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogeny</a>, and these studies indicate that bacteria diverged first from the archaeal/eukaryotic lineage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> of bacteria and archaea was probably a <a href="/wiki/Thermophile" title="Thermophile">hyperthermophile</a> that lived about 2.5 billion–3.2 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria were also involved in the second great evolutionary divergence, that of the archaea and eukaryotes. Here, eukaryotes resulted from the entering of ancient bacteria into <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiotic</a> associations with the ancestors of eukaryotic cells, which were themselves possibly related to the <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyall_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dyall-27">[27]</a></sup> This involved the engulfment by proto-eukaryotic cells of <a href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacteria" title="Alphaproteobacteria">alphaproteobacterial</a> <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbionts</a> to form either <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenosome" title="Hydrogenosome">hydrogenosomes</a>, which are still found in all known Eukarya (sometimes in highly <a href="/wiki/Reduced_form" title="Reduced form">reduced form</a>, e.g. in ancient "amitochondrial" protozoa). Later, some eukaryotes that already contained mitochondria also engulfed <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>-like organisms, leading to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> in algae and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This is known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_endosymbiosis" title="Secondary endosymbiosis">secondary endosymbiosis</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cell_structure#Cell_morphology" title="Bacterial cell structure">Bacterial cell structure § Cell morphology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg"><img alt="a diagram showing bacteria morphology" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="825" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg/350px-Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg/525px-Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg/700px-Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bacterial_morphology_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bacteria display many cell <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologies</a> and arrangements</div></div></div>
<p>Bacteria display a wide diversity of shapes and sizes, called <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologies</a>. Bacterial cells are about one-tenth the size of eukaryotic cells and are typically 0.5–5.0 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometres</a> in length. However, a few species are visible to the unaided eye—for example, <i><a href="/wiki/Thiomargarita_namibiensis" title="Thiomargarita namibiensis">Thiomargarita namibiensis</a></i> is up to half a millimetre long<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Epulopiscium_fishelsoni" title="Epulopiscium fishelsoni">Epulopiscium fishelsoni</a></i> reaches 0.7 mm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2011_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2011-31">[31]</a></sup> Among the smallest bacteria are members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma" title="Mycoplasma">Mycoplasma</a></i>, which measure only 0.3 micrometres, as small as the largest <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Some bacteria may be even smaller, but these <a href="/wiki/Ultramicrobacteria" title="Ultramicrobacteria">ultramicrobacteria</a> are not well-studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Velimirov2001_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Velimirov2001-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most bacterial species are either spherical, called <i><a href="/wiki/Coccus" title="Coccus">cocci</a></i> (<i>sing</i>. coccus, from Greek <i>kókkos</i>, grain, seed), or rod-shaped, called <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_(shape)" title="Bacillus (shape)">bacilli</a></i> (<i>sing</i>. bacillus, from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>baculus</i>, stick).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Some bacteria, called <i><a href="/wiki/Spiral_bacteria#Vibrio" title="Spiral bacteria">vibrio</a></i>, are shaped like slightly curved rods or comma-shaped; others can be spiral-shaped, called <i><a href="/wiki/Spiral_bacteria#Spirillum" title="Spiral bacteria">spirilla</a></i>, or tightly coiled, called <i><a href="/wiki/Spiral_bacteria#Spirochete" title="Spiral bacteria">spirochaetes</a></i>. A small number of other unusual shapes have been described, such as star-shaped bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This wide variety of shapes is determined by the bacterial <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cytoskeleton" title="Cytoskeleton">cytoskeleton</a>, and is important because it can influence the ability of bacteria to acquire nutrients, attach to surfaces, swim through liquids and escape <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relative_scale.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="936" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Relative_scale.svg/220px-Relative_scale.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Relative_scale.svg/330px-Relative_scale.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Relative_scale.svg/440px-Relative_scale.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relative_scale.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The range of sizes shown by <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>, relative to those of other organisms and <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Many bacterial species exist simply as single cells, others associate in characteristic patterns: <i><a href="/wiki/Neisseria" title="Neisseria">Neisseria</a></i> form diploids (pairs), <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus" title="Streptococcus">Streptococcus</a></i> form chains, and <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus" title="Staphylococcus">Staphylococcus</a></i> group together in "bunch of grapes" clusters. Bacteria can also group to form larger multicellular structures, such as the elongated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filamentous_bacteria" title="Filamentous bacteria">filaments</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Actinobacteria" title="Actinobacteria">Actinobacteria</a></i>, the aggregates of <i><a href="/wiki/Myxobacteria" title="Myxobacteria">Myxobacteria</a></i>, and the complex hyphae of <i><a href="/wiki/Streptomyces" title="Streptomyces">Streptomyces</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> These multicellular structures are often only seen in certain conditions. For example, when starved of amino acids, <a href="/wiki/Myxobacteria" title="Myxobacteria">Myxobacteria</a> detect surrounding cells in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Quorum_sensing" title="Quorum sensing">quorum sensing</a>, migrate towards each other, and aggregate to form fruiting bodies up to 500 micrometres long and containing approximately 100,000 bacterial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In these fruiting bodies, the bacteria perform separate tasks; for example, about one in ten cells migrate to the top of a fruiting body and differentiate into a specialised dormant state called a myxospore, which is more resistant to drying and other adverse environmental conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaiser_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaiser-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria often attach to surfaces and form dense aggregations called <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilms</a>, and larger formations known as <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mats</a>. These biofilms and mats can range from a few micrometres in thickness to up to half a metre in depth, and may contain multiple species of bacteria, <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>. Bacteria living in biofilms display a complex arrangement of cells and extracellular components, forming secondary structures, such as <a href="/wiki/Microcolony" title="Microcolony">microcolonies</a>, through which there are networks of channels to enable better diffusion of nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In natural environments, such as soil or the surfaces of plants, the majority of bacteria are bound to surfaces in biofilms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davey_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davey-43">[43]</a></sup> Biofilms are also important in medicine, as these structures are often present during chronic bacterial infections or in infections of <a href="/wiki/Implant_(medicine)" title="Implant (medicine)">implanted</a> <a href="/wiki/Medical_device" title="Medical device">medical devices</a>, and bacteria protected within biofilms are much harder to kill than individual isolated bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cellular_structure">Cellular structure</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cell_structure" title="Bacterial cell structure">Bacterial cell structure</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prokaryote_cell.svg"><img alt="Prokaryote cell with structure and parts" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="910" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Prokaryote_cell.svg/220px-Prokaryote_cell.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Prokaryote_cell.svg/330px-Prokaryote_cell.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Prokaryote_cell.svg/440px-Prokaryote_cell.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prokaryote_cell.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure and contents of a typical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-positive" title="Gram-positive">gram-positive</a> bacterial cell (seen by the fact that only <i>one</i> cell membrane is present).</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intracellular_structures">Intracellular structures</span></h3>
<p>The bacterial cell is surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> which is made primarily of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>. This membrane encloses the contents of the cell and acts as a barrier to hold nutrients, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and other essential components of the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> within the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Unlike <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic cells</a>, bacteria usually lack large membrane-bound structures in their cytoplasm such as a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> and the other organelles present in eukaryotic cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stryer_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stryer-46">[46]</a></sup> However, some bacteria have protein-bound organelles in the cytoplasm which compartmentalize aspects of bacterial metabolism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bobik2006_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bobik2006-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Carboxysome" title="Carboxysome">carboxysome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Additionally, bacteria have a multi-component <a href="/wiki/Prokaryotic_cytoskeleton" title="Prokaryotic cytoskeleton">cytoskeleton</a> to control the localisation of proteins and nucleic acids within the cell, and to manage the process of <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gitai_Z_2005_577–86_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gitai_Z_2005_577–86-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many important <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> reactions, such as energy generation, occur due to <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">concentration gradients</a> across membranes, creating a <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_potential" title="Electrochemical potential">potential</a> difference analogous to a battery. The general lack of internal membranes in bacteria means these reactions, such as <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport</a>, occur across the cell membrane between the cytoplasm and the outside of the cell or <a href="/wiki/Periplasm" title="Periplasm">periplasm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> However, in many photosynthetic bacteria the plasma membrane is highly folded and fills most of the cell with layers of light-gathering membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryantfrigaard_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-bryantfrigaard-54">[54]</a></sup> These light-gathering complexes may even form lipid-enclosed structures called <a href="/wiki/Chlorosome" title="Chlorosome">chlorosomes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Green_sulfur_bacteria" title="Green sulfur bacteria">green sulfur bacteria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="911" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg/220px-Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg/330px-Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg/440px-Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carboxysomes_EM_ptA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_microscopy" title="Electron microscopy">electron micrograph</a> of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halothiobacillus_neapolitanus" title="Halothiobacillus neapolitanus">Halothiobacillus neapolitanus</a></i> cells with carboxysomes inside, with arrows highlighting visible carboxysomes. Scale bars indicate 100 nm.</div></div></div>
<p>Most bacteria do not have a membrane-bound nucleus, and their <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetic</a> material is typically a single <a href="/wiki/Circular_bacterial_chromosome" title="Circular bacterial chromosome">circular bacterial chromosome</a> of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> located in the cytoplasm in an irregularly shaped body called the <a href="/wiki/Nucleoid" title="Nucleoid">nucleoid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The nucleoid contains the <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a> with its associated proteins and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>. Like all <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">living organisms</a>, bacteria contain <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> for the production of proteins, but the structure of the bacterial ribosome is different from that of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some bacteria produce intracellular nutrient storage granules, such as <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polyphosphate" title="Polyphosphate">polyphosphate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Polyhydroxyalkanoates" title="Polyhydroxyalkanoates">polyhydroxyalkanoates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Certain bacterial species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">Cyanobacteria</a>, produce internal <a href="/wiki/Vacuole#Bacteria" title="Vacuole">gas vacuoles</a> which they use to regulate their buoyancy, allowing them to move up or down into water layers with different light intensities and nutrient levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extracellular_structures">Extracellular structures</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cell_envelope" title="Cell envelope">Cell envelope</a></div>
<p>Around the outside of the cell membrane is the <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a>. Bacterial cell walls are made of <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a> (called "murein" in older sources), which is made from <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a> chains cross-linked by <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptides</a> containing D-<a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Bacterial cell walls are different from the cell walls of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, which are made of <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koch_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koch-64">[64]</a></sup> The cell wall of bacteria is also distinct from that of Archaea, which do not contain peptidoglycan. The cell wall is essential to the survival of many bacteria, and the antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a> is able to kill bacteria by inhibiting a step in the synthesis of peptidoglycan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koch_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koch-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are broadly speaking two different types of cell wall in bacteria, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-positive" title="Gram-positive">Gram-positive</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-negative" title="Gram-negative">Gram-negative</a>. The names originate from the reaction of cells to the <a href="/wiki/Gram_stain" title="Gram stain">Gram stain</a>, a long-standing test for the classification of bacterial species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gram_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gram-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gram-positive bacteria possess a thick cell wall containing many layers of peptidoglycan and <a href="/wiki/Teichoic_acid" title="Teichoic acid">teichoic acids</a>. In contrast, Gram-negative bacteria have a relatively thin cell wall consisting of a few layers of peptidoglycan surrounded by a second <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid membrane</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Lipopolysaccharide" title="Lipopolysaccharide">lipopolysaccharides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipoprotein" title="Lipoprotein">lipoproteins</a>. Most bacteria have the Gram-negative cell wall, and only the <a href="/wiki/Firmicutes" title="Firmicutes">Firmicutes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinobacteria" title="Actinobacteria">Actinobacteria</a> (previously known as the low G+C and high G+C gram-positive bacteria, respectively) have the alternative Gram-positive arrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> These differences in structure can produce differences in antibiotic susceptibility; for instance, <a href="/wiki/Vancomycin" title="Vancomycin">vancomycin</a> can kill only Gram-positive bacteria and is ineffective against Gram-negative <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Some bacteria have cell wall structures that are neither classically Gram-positive or Gram-negative. This includes clinically important bacteria such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycobacteria" title="Mycobacteria">Mycobacteria</a></i> which have a thick peptidoglycan cell wall like a Gram-positive bacterium, but also a second outer layer of lipids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many bacteria, an <a href="/wiki/S-layer" title="S-layer">S-layer</a> of rigidly arrayed protein molecules covers the outside of the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> This layer provides chemical and physical protection for the cell surface and can act as a <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecular</a> <a href="/wiki/Diffusion_barrier" title="Diffusion barrier">diffusion barrier</a>. S-layers have diverse but mostly poorly understood functions, but are known to act as virulence factors in <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter" title="Campylobacter">Campylobacter</a></i> and contain surface <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacillus_stearothermophilus" title="Bacillus stearothermophilus">Bacillus stearothermophilus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EMpylori.jpg"><img alt="Helicobacter pylori electron micrograph, showing multiple flagella on the cell surface" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/EMpylori.jpg/220px-EMpylori.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/EMpylori.jpg/330px-EMpylori.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/EMpylori.jpg/440px-EMpylori.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EMpylori.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Helicobacter_pylori" title="Helicobacter pylori">Helicobacter pylori</a></i> electron micrograph, showing multiple flagella on the cell surface</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">Flagella</a> are rigid protein structures, about 20 nanometres in diameter and up to 20 micrometres in length, that are used for <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motility</a>. Flagella are driven by the energy released by the transfer of <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> down an <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradient</a> across the cell membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fimbria_(bacteriology)" title="Fimbria (bacteriology)">Fimbriae</a> (sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Pilus#Fimbriae" title="Pilus">attachment pili</a>") are fine filaments of protein, usually 2–10 nanometres in diameter and up to several micrometres in length. They are distributed over the surface of the cell, and resemble fine hairs when seen under the <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscope</a>. Fimbriae are believed to be involved in attachment to solid surfaces or to other cells, and are essential for the virulence of some bacterial pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pilus" title="Pilus">Pili</a> (<i>sing</i>. pilus) are cellular appendages, slightly larger than fimbriae, that can transfer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_material" title="Genetic material">genetic material</a> between bacterial cells in a process called <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_conjugation" title="Bacterial conjugation">conjugation</a> where they are called <a href="/wiki/Pilus#Conjugative_pili" title="Pilus">conjugation pili</a> or sex pili (see bacterial genetics, below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> They can also generate movement where they are called <a href="/wiki/Pilus#type_iv_pili" title="Pilus">type IV pili</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Glycocalyx#Glycocalyx_in_Bacteria_and_Nature" title="Glycocalyx">Glycocalyx</a> is produced by many bacteria to surround their cells, and varies in structural complexity: ranging from a disorganised <a href="/wiki/Slime_layer" title="Slime layer">slime layer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_polymeric_substance" title="Extracellular polymeric substance">extracellular polymeric substances</a> to a highly structured <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_capsule" title="Bacterial capsule">capsule</a>. These structures can protect cells from engulfment by eukaryotic cells such as <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> (part of the human <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> They can also act as <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> and be involved in cell recognition, as well as aiding attachment to surfaces and the formation of biofilms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The assembly of these extracellular structures is dependent on <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_secretion_system" title="Bacterial secretion system">bacterial secretion systems</a>. These transfer proteins from the cytoplasm into the periplasm or into the environment around the cell. Many types of secretion systems are known and these structures are often essential for the <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> of pathogens, so are intensively studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endospores">Endospores</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Endospore" title="Endospore">Endospore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg"><img alt="Anthrax stained purple" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="600" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg/220px-Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg/330px-Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg/440px-Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gram_Stain_Anthrax.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i> (stained purple) growing in <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">cerebrospinal fluid</a></div></div></div>
<p>Certain <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> of Gram-positive bacteria, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus" title="Bacillus">Bacillus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium" title="Clostridium">Clostridium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sporohalobacter" title="Sporohalobacter">Sporohalobacter</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Anaerobacter" title="Anaerobacter">Anaerobacter</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Heliobacteria" title="Heliobacteria">Heliobacterium</a></i>, can form highly resistant, dormant structures called <i><a href="/wiki/Endospore" title="Endospore">endospores</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Endospores develop within the cytoplasm of the cell; generally a single endospore develops in each cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenney_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKenney-79">[79]</a></sup> Each endospore contains a core of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> surrounded by a cortex layer and protected by a multilayer rigid coat composed of peptidoglycan and a variety of proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenney_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKenney-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Endospores show no detectable <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> and can survive extreme physical and chemical stresses, such as high levels of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV light</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma radiation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disinfectant" title="Disinfectant">disinfectants</a>, heat, freezing, pressure, and <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In this dormant state, these organisms may remain viable for millions of years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and endospores even allow bacteria to survive exposure to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_vacuum" title="Hard vacuum">vacuum</a> and radiation in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Endospore-forming bacteria can also cause disease: for example, <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> can be contracted by the inhalation of <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i> endospores, and contamination of deep puncture wounds with <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_tetani" title="Clostridium tetani">Clostridium tetani</a></i> endospores causes <a href="/wiki/Tetanus" title="Tetanus">tetanus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Microbial_metabolism" title="Microbial metabolism">Microbial metabolism</a></div>
<p>Bacteria exhibit an extremely wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The distribution of metabolic traits within a group of bacteria has traditionally been used to define their <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>, but these traits often do not correspond with modern genetic classifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Bacterial metabolism is classified into <a href="/wiki/Primary_nutritional_groups" title="Primary nutritional groups">nutritional groups</a> on the basis of three major criteria: the source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_(biology)" title="Energy (biology)">energy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">electron donors</a> used, and the source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> used for growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacteria either derive energy from light using <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> (called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phototrophy" title="Phototrophy">phototrophy</a>), or by breaking down chemical compounds using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> (called <a href="/wiki/Chemotroph" title="Chemotroph">chemotrophy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicroMetab_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-MicroMetab-88">[88]</a></sup> Chemotrophs use chemical compounds as a source of energy by transferring electrons from a given electron donor to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_electron_acceptor" title="Terminal electron acceptor">terminal electron acceptor</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox reaction</a>. This reaction releases energy that can be used to drive metabolism. Chemotrophs are further divided by the types of compounds they use to transfer electrons. Bacteria that use inorganic compounds such as hydrogren, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> as <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">sources of electrons</a> are called <a href="/wiki/Lithotroph" title="Lithotroph">lithotrophs</a>, while those that use organic compounds are called <a href="/wiki/Organotroph" title="Organotroph">organotrophs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicroMetab_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-MicroMetab-88">[88]</a></sup> The compounds used to receive electrons are also used to classify bacteria: <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a> use <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> as the terminal electron acceptor, while <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic organisms</a> use other compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a>, or carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicroMetab_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-MicroMetab-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many bacteria get their carbon from other <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic carbon</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophy</a>. Others such as <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Purple_bacteria" title="Purple bacteria">purple bacteria</a> are <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">autotrophic</a>, meaning that they obtain cellular carbon by <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">fixing</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In unusual circumstances, the gas <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> can be used by <a href="/wiki/Methanotroph" title="Methanotroph">methanotrophic</a> bacteria as both a source of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and a substrate for carbon <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;">

</th></tr>
<tr>
<td> <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">Phototrophs</a> 
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">Sunlight
</td>
<td style="text-align:center;"> Organic compounds (photoheterotrophs) or carbon fixation (photoautotrophs)
</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr>

</td>
